We have not yet fully characterized all N that are fixed points. In our earlier derivation, we assumed N = 6k, and condition for fixed point is that the three largest proper divisors of N are N/2, N/3, N/6. So we need to characterize N such that the set of proper divisors > N/6 is exactly {N/2, N/3}. This is what we analyzed for N=6k, but we need to consider the full factorization.

Let's set N = 6k. Then the divisors of N are of the form 2^α 3^β d, where d|k, and α,β are integers such that 0≤α≤v2(N)=1+v2(k), 0≤β≤v3(N)=1+v3(k). But we are interested in divisors > k. Since k = N/6, and N/2 = 3k, N/3 = 2k. So we need that any divisor D of N with D > k is either 2k or 3k (and D < N, so D proper). So the condition is: For any divisor D of N, if D > k then D ∈ {2k,3k}. Since D is a divisor, D = 2^α 3^β d, with d|k. Then we need to analyze.

We can write k = 2^c 3^d m, with m coprime to 6. Then N = 2^{c+1} 3^{d+1} m. Divisors D = 2^α 3^β t, where 0≤α≤c+1, 0≤β≤d+1, t|m. We need that if D > k = 2^c 3^d m, then D = 2k = 2^{c+1} 3^d m, or D = 3k = 2^c 3^{d+1} m. Note that 2k and 3k are themselves of that form: 2k corresponds to α=c+1, β=d, t=m; 3k corresponds to α=c, β=d+1, t=m.

Now, we need to ensure that no other combination yields a divisor D with D > k.

Consider D = 2^α 3^β t. For D to be > k, we need 2^α 3^β t > 2^c 3^d m. Since t|m, we can compare.

Let's denote the exponent difference: Let α' = α - c, β' = β - d. Then D > k iff 2^{α'} 3^{β'} t > m. But note that t can be any divisor of m. Also, the range of α,β: α from 0 to c+1, so α' from -c to 1; similarly β' from -d to 1. However, we must have D integer and also the total exponents are limited. But we can think in terms of the factorization.

The condition that the only divisors > k are 2k and 3k implies that for any divisor D of N, if D > k then D = 2k or D = 3k. So we can try to characterize all N with this property.

Now, consider c=1,d=1 (k=2*3=6). Then N = 2^{2} 3^{2} m = 36m. Divisors: 2^α 3^β t, with α=0,1,2; β=0,1,2; t|m. We need that the only divisors > k = 6m are 2k=12m and 3k=18m. But 9t with t=7 gives 63>42. So the divisor 63 is a proper divisor > k. And 63 is not 2k=84 nor 3k=126. So fails. So c=1,d=1 fails.

Thus we need to characterize all N that are fixed points. In general, N = 2^{c+1} 3^{d+1} m, with m coprime to 6. The condition for fixed point is that the only proper divisors > N/6 = k = 2^c 3^d m are exactly 2k and 3k. This is a specific condition on the exponents c,d and the integer m.

We can attempt to find all N that satisfy this. Let's denote the prime factorization of N. Since N is a multiple of 6, we have N = 2^a 3^b * M, where M is coprime to 6. Here a = c+1, b = d+1. Then k = N/6 = 2^{a-1} 3^{b-1} M. The condition: For any divisor D of N, if D > k then D = 2k or D = 3k. Since 2k = 2^a 3^{b-1} M, 3k = 2^{a-1} 3^b M.

Now, consider divisors of N. They are of the form 2^α 3^β * t, where 0≤α≤a, 0≤β≤b, and t|M. We need to ensure that no combination other than (α=a,β=b-1,t=M) and (α=a-1,β=b,t=M) yields a divisor > k.

Let's analyze the possible divisors D = 2^α 3^β t. Compare with k = 2^{a-1} 3^{b-1} M. For D to be > k, we need 2^{α-(a-1)} 3^{β-(b-1)} t > M. Let’s define A = α - (a-1), B = β - (b-1). Then D > k iff 2^A 3^B t > M. The ranges: α from 0 to a, so A from -(a-1) to 1; similarly B from -(b-1) to 1. The exponents can be negative.

The two allowed divisors are: (α=a,β=b-1,t=M) gives A=1, B=0, t=M, so 2^1 * 3^0 * M = 2M, which is > M? Actually, 2M > M, so D > k. And (α=a-1,β=b,t=M) gives A=0, B=1, t=M, so 2^0 * 3^1 * M = 3M, > M. So these are the ones.

Now, we need to ensure that no other combination yields D > k. In particular, consider t = M itself (the largest divisor of M). Then D = 2^α 3^β M. For D to be > k, we need 2^α 3^β > 2^{a-1} 3^{b-1} = k/M? Actually, k = 2^{a-1} 3^{b-1} M, so D > k iff 2^α 3^β > 2^{a-1} 3^{b-1}. That is, (α,β) in the set of exponents that give a product > 2^{a-1} 3^{b-1}. Since α ≤ a, β ≤ b. The maximum possible product from other exponents is when α=a, β=b gives 2^a 3^b, which is N/M. But that's N itself (since t=M). So D = N is not proper. So we need to consider (α,β) such that 2^α 3^β M is a proper divisor, i.e., < N. That means (α,β) ≠ (a,b). So the allowed (α,β) with (α,β) ≠ (a,b) and 2^α 3^β > 2^{a-1} 3^{b-1} would produce D > k, D ≠ N. So we need to ensure that the only (α,β) with (α,β) ≠ (a,b) that satisfy 2^α 3^β > 2^{a-1} 3^{b-1} are exactly (α=a,β=b-1) and (α=a-1,β=b). Are there any other? Let's analyze.

We have the lattice of exponents (α,β) with 0≤α≤a, 0≤β≤b. The condition 2^α 3^β > 2^{a-1} 3^{b-1} is equivalent to 2^{α-(a-1)} 3^{β-(b-1)} > 1. That is, (α,β) such that the product of powers is >1. Since 2 and 3 are relatively prime, the set of points (α,β) that give product >1 is the set of points that are not in the "lower left" region where α ≤ a-1 and β ≤ b-1? But we need to consider the boundary.

Define the threshold: The line where 2^α 3^β = 2^{a-1} 3^{b-1} is not linear in the exponent plane because it's exponential. However, we can think in terms of the integer comparison.

Let’s consider the case a=1,b=1 (N=2*3*M=6M). Then k = 2^{0}3^{0}M = M. The allowed (α,β) with t=M: (α,β) = (1,0) gives 2M = 2k; (0,1) gives 3M = 3k; (1,1) gives 6M = N, not proper; (0,0) gives M=k. So the only divisors >k are 2k and 3k. So for a=1,b=1, the condition from t=M is automatically satisfied, because any other (α,β) gives product ≤? For example, (α=0,β=0) gives M=k, not >; (α=1,β=0) and (0,1) are allowed. So no other (α,β) gives >k. So the critical case is when M is not 1, we also have to consider t|M with t<M. For a=1,b=1, we have N=6M. Divisors D = 2^α 3^β t, with t|M. For D>k=M, we need 2^α 3^β t > M. Since t|M, t ≤ M. The possibilities: α=1,β=0 gives D=2t. For D>M, we need t > M/2. Since t|M, and M is coprime to 6, the only t > M/2 is t=M (since any proper divisor > M/2 would give M/t < 2, so M/t=1 => t=M). So D=2M=2k, allowed. α=0,β=1 gives D=3t. For D>M, need t > M/3. Since M coprime to 6, the only t > M/3 is t=M? Because if t > M/3, then M/t < 3. Could be 2 or 1. But M/t=2 would give t=M/2, not integer if M odd? M is coprime to 6, so M is odd and not divisible by 3. So M/2 not integer. So only t=M. So D=3M=3k, allowed. α=1,β=1 gives D=6t. For D>M, need 6t > M => t > M/6. Since t|M, t can be M itself (then D=6M=N, not proper) or proper divisors t with t > M/6. So that would be a divisor > M other than 2M or 3M. So condition fails if 5|M. If M not divisible by 5, then no proper t > M/6 exists, so the only D>M from (α,β)=(1,1) are N itself (t=M). So condition holds if M not divisible by 5. That matches our earlier family: N=6m with m coprime to 6 and not divisible by 5.

Thus the general condition is: N = 2^a 3^b * M, with M coprime to 6. Then the condition for fixed point is that for all t|M with t<M, and for all (α,β) such that D=2^α 3^β t is a proper divisor (D<N), we need D ≤ k = 2^{a-1} 3^{b-1} M, except when D=2k or 3k. The allowed D=2k and 3k are themselves of the form 2^a 3^{b-1} M and 2^{a-1} 3^b M, which correspond to (α,β,t) = (a,b-1,M) and (a-1,b,M). So we need to avoid any other combination that yields a divisor > k.

In general, the divisors of N can be classified by the exponent pair (α,β) and the divisor t of M. The condition that a divisor D = 2^α 3^β t is a proper divisor means that either (α,β) ≠ (a,b) or t ≠ M. Since N = 2^a 3^b M, the largest proper divisor is N/2 = 2^{a-1} 3^b M, which is 3k? Actually, k = 2^{a-1} 3^{b-1} M, so N/2 = 2^{a-1} 3^b M = 3k, and N/3 = 2^a 3^{b-1} M = 2k. So these are the two largest proper divisors.

Now, we need to check whether any other divisor can exceed k. That is, for any (α,β,t) with D = 2^α 3^β t, D < N, and D > k.

We can think of the ratio D/k = 2^{α-(a-1)} 3^{β-(b-1)} t/M. Let's define u = α-(a-1), v = β-(b-1). Then D/k = 2^u 3^v * (t/M). We want D/k > 1, and we want that the only possibilities for D/k > 1 with D<N are when D/k = 2 (i.e., u=1,v=0,t=M) or D/k = 3 (u=0,v=1,t=M). So we need that for any other combination, D/k ≤ 1.

Thus the condition is: For all (α,β,t) with D = 2^α 3^β t < N, we have 2^{α-(a-1)} 3^{β-(b-1)} t/M ≤ 1, except when (α,β,t) = (a,b-1,M) or (a-1,b,M) where it equals 2 or 3.

Now, note that if t < M, then t/M < 1, so the product may be ≤1 even if 2^u 3^v is slightly >1. But we need to ensure that for no (α,β,t) with t<M does the product exceed 1. So we need to bound the possible 2^u 3^v for given α,β ranges.

Since α ranges 0..a, u ranges -(a-1)..1. Similarly v ranges -(b-1)..1. The maximum possible 2^u 3^v for u,v in that range is when u=1, v=1 gives 6, but that's allowed only if t=M and we get D=N, not proper. So we need to consider u=1, v=1, t<M: then D/k = 6 * (t/M). For D/k >1, we need t > M/6. So if there exists a proper divisor t of M with t > M/6, then D = 2^a 3^b t? Actually, (α,β)=(a,b) gives D=2^a 3^b t, but that's not proper if t<M? Wait, (α,β)=(a,b) with t<M gives D = 2^a 3^b t = (2^a 3^b M) * (t/M) = N * (t/M). Since t<M, D<N. So that is a proper divisor. And D/k = (2^a 3^b t) / (2^{a-1} 3^{b-1} M) = 2 * 3 * (t/M) = 6*(t/M). So D/k = 6*(t/M). For D/k >1, we need t > M/6. So if M has a proper divisor t with t > M/6, then D = N * (t/M) is a proper divisor > k. So condition fails. Thus we must have that for every proper divisor t of M, t ≤ M/6. As argued, for M coprime to 6, this is equivalent to M not being divisible by 5, because the only possible t > M/6 is t = M/5 (if 5|M). So M must not be divisible by 5. This is necessary.

Now, also consider other combinations like u=1, v=0, t<M: then D/k = 2 * (t/M). For D/k >1, need t > M/2. As argued, no proper t > M/2 exists because any proper divisor > M/2 would force M/t < 2, so M/t=1 => t=M. So no proper t > M/2. So safe.

Similarly, u=0, v=1, t<M: D/k = 3*(t/M). For D/k >1, need t > M/3. As argued, if M is coprime to 6, the only possible t > M/3 is t=M (since M/t could be 2, but M even? M odd, so M/t=2 impossible). So no proper t > M/3. So safe.

Now, consider u=1, v=0, t=M? That's allowed. u=0, v=1, t=M allowed. u=1, v=1, t=M gives D=N, not proper. So the only problematic case is from (α,β)=(a,b) with t proper divisor of M, which gives D = N * (t/M). So the condition is that M has no proper divisor t > M/6. That is, the only divisor of M that is greater than M/6 is M itself. As we argued, for M coprime to 6, this holds if and only if M is not divisible by 5. Because if M has a prime factor p < 6, i.e., p=2,3,5, then M/p is a proper divisor > M/6 if p=5? Let's check: For p=2, M even, but M coprime to 6, so cannot be even. So M must be coprime to 6 and not divisible by 5.

But wait, we also need to consider the possibility that (α,β) are not at the maximum but still could give D > k. For example, consider u=0, v=0, t<M: D/k = t/M < 1, so safe. Consider u= -1, v=1? That would be α=a-2, β=b? But we need to consider all (α,β). The general approach is to note that any divisor D of N can be written as 2^α 3^β t. The condition D > k is equivalent to 2^{α-(a-1)} 3^{β-(b-1)} t > M. Since t ≤ M, and the factor 2^{α-(a-1)} 3^{β-(b-1)} is a rational number that can be less than 1 if α ≤ a-2 or β ≤ b-2. So the only way to get >1 is if α is large and β is large, i.e., α = a or a-1, β = b or b-1, etc. So the critical cases are when α is near a and β near b. We already considered (α,β) = (a,b) with t proper. Also, consider (α,β) = (a,b-1) with t proper: then D = 2^a 3^{b-1} t. Compare with k = 2^{a-1} 3^{b-1} M. Then D/k = 2 * (t/M). (α,β) = (a,b) with t=M gives D=N, not proper. So the only possible source of violation is from (α,β) = (a,b) with t proper. So the condition reduces to: M has no proper divisor t > M/6.

Thus the fixed points are exactly all numbers N such that N = 2^a 3^b * M, where M is coprime to 6 and M has no proper divisor > M/6. Since M is coprime to 6, the condition that M has no proper divisor > M/6 is equivalent to M not being divisible by 5. But is that sufficient? Let's test M=49 (7^2). M=49, proper divisors: 1,7. So we need to also ensure that for all (α,β) with α ≤ a, β ≤ b, and t|M, the only D > k are 2k and 3k. In particular, consider (α,β) = (a-2,b) or (a,b-2) etc. So we need to analyze the full set.

Thus the fixed point condition is more restrictive. We need to find all N such that the set of proper divisors > N/6 is exactly {N/2, N/3}. Let's analyze systematically.

Let N = 2^a 3^b M, with M coprime to 6. Let k = N/6 = 2^{a-1} 3^{b-1} M. The divisors of N are all numbers of the form 2^α 3^β t, where 0≤α≤a, 0≤β≤b, t|M. We need that for any such divisor D with D < N, if D > k then D ∈ {2k,3k}. Note that 2k = 2^a 3^{b-1} M, 3k = 2^{a-1} 3^b M.

Now, consider the possible pairs (α,β) and t. For D to be > k, we need 2^{α-(a-1)} 3^{β-(b-1)} t > M. Since t ≤ M, the only way this can happen is if the factor 2^{α-(a-1)} 3^{β-(b-1)} is sufficiently large. In particular, if α ≤ a-2 and β ≤ b-2, then 2^{α-(a-1)} 3^{β-(b-1)} ≤ 2^{-1} 3^{-1} = 1/6, so the product is at most (1/6) * (t/M) ≤ 1/6, so D/k ≤ 1/6 < 1. So no problem. If α = a and β = b-1, then factor = 2^{1} 3^{0}=2, so D/k = 2*(t/M). For D/k >1, need t > M/2. As argued, no proper t > M/2 exists. So the only chance is t=M, giving D=2k. Similarly, α = a-1, β = b gives D/k = 3*(t/M). For D/k >1, need t > M/3, which only happens if t=M, giving D=3k. So those are allowed.

Now, consider α = a, β = b-1, but t proper? No, because D/k = 2*(t/M) ≤ 2*(1/2)=1 if t≤M/2? Actually, if t < M/2, then D/k < 1, so D ≤ k. So no violation.

Consider α = a-1, β = b-1: factor = 2^{-1} 3^{0} = 1/2? Actually, α-(a-1) = -1, β-(b-1)=0, so factor = 2^{-1}=1/2. Then D/k = (1/2)*(t/M). Since t/M ≤ 1, D/k ≤ 1/2 < 1. So safe.

Consider α = a, β = b-2: factor = 2^{1} 3^{-1}=2/3. Then D/k = (2/3)*(t/M). For D/k >1, need t > (3/2)M, impossible since t≤M. So safe.

Consider α = a-2, β = b: factor = 2^{-2} 3^{1}=3/4. D/k = (3/4)*(t/M) ≤ 3/4 <1. Safe.

Consider α = a-2, β = b-1: factor = 2^{-2} 3^{0}=1/4. Safe.

Consider α = a-1, β = b-2: factor = 2^{-1} 3^{-1}=1/6. Safe.

Now, consider α = a, β = b: factor = 2^{1} 3^{1}=6. Then D/k = 6*(t/M). For D/k >1, need t > M/6. So if there exists a proper divisor t of M with t > M/6, then D = 2^a 3^b t = N * (t/M) is a proper divisor > k. So that would violate. Thus we need that M has no proper divisor t > M/6. As we argued, for M coprime to 6, this means M is not divisible by 5. However, we also have to consider the case where (α,β) = (a-1,b-1) with t? That's safe. But what about (α,β) = (a,b-1) with t proper? Already considered: D/k = 2*(t/M) ≤ 1 if t≤M/2, so safe. (α,β) = (a-1,b) with t proper: D/k = 3*(t/M) ≤ 1 if t≤M/3, safe.

But wait, we also need to consider the possibility that α and β are not at the extremes but t is large enough to make the product >1. For instance, consider α = a, β = b-2, t = M? Then D/k = (2^1 3^{-1}) * M/M = 2/3 <1. So safe. α = a-2, β = b, t = M? Then D/k = (2^{-2}3^1)=3/4 <1. Safe. α = a-2, β = b-1, t = M? D/k = 2^{-2}3^0=1/4. Safe. α = a-1, β = b-2, t = M? D/k = 2^{-1}3^{-1}=1/6. Safe. So only the (a,b) combination with t proper could produce D/k >1, and possibly also (a,b-1) with t proper? That gave 2*(t/M). If t is close to M, say t = M/2? But M/2 not integer because M odd. So t cannot be M/2. t could be M/3? M/3 not integer because M not divisible by 3. So t is at most M/7? Actually, if M is not divisible by 5, the largest proper divisor could be M/7 if 7|M, but then t=M/7, then 2*(M/7)/M = 2/7 < 1. So safe. So indeed the only possible source of violation is from (α,β) = (a,b) with t proper divisor > M/6.

But earlier we found a counterexample with N=1764 (a=2,b=2,M=49). For M=49, proper divisors: 1,7. M/6≈8.166, so 7<8.166, so no proper divisor > M/6. So according to this, the condition from (α,β)=(a,b) with t proper should be safe because t=7 gives D/k = 6*(7/49)=6*(1/7)=6/7<1, so D<k. Indeed, D=2^2*3^2*7=4*9*7=252, k=2^{1}3^{1}*49=2*3*49=294, so D=252<294. So that's not a violation. But we found a divisor 441>294 from (α,β)=(0,2) with t=49. Let's check: (α,β)=(0,2) gives D=2^0 3^2 *49 = 9*49=441. That is of the form (α,β)=(0,2) and t=M=49. For this D, we have α-(a-1)=0-(2-1)=-1, β-(b-1)=2-(2-1)=1, so factor = 2^{-1} 3^1 = 3/2? Actually, 2^{-1} * 3^1 = 3/2. Then D/k = (3/2)*(t/M) = (3/2)*(49/49)=3/2 >1. So this is a case where (α,β) is not (a,b), but still D/k >1 because the factor 2^{α-(a-1)} 3^{β-(b-1)} can be >1 even if α ≤ a-2? For (α,β)=(0,2) with a=2,b=2, we have α-(a-1)= -1, β-(b-1)=1, so factor = 2^{-1}*3^1=3/2>1. So indeed, the factor can be >1 if the exponent differences are not both negative, i.e., if β is large enough to compensate for α being small. So we need to consider all (α,β) such that 2^{α-(a-1)} 3^{β-(b-1)} > 1, i.e., the product of powers is >1. Since 2 and 3 are >1, this requires that the net exponent of 2 and 3 in the factor is positive in a certain sense. The condition is that α-(a-1) and β-(b-1) can be negative or positive, but the product 2^{α-(a-1)} 3^{β-(b-1)} > 1. This is equivalent to α * log2 + β * log3 > (a-1)*log2 + (b-1)*log3. Since log2 and log3 are incommensurable, we cannot simply use exponent comparisons; we need to consider the actual integer values.

So the general approach is: For each divisor D = 2^α 3^β t with t|M, we need that if D > k, then D = 2k or 3k. So we need to characterize all N such that the only divisors D > N/6 are N/2 and N/3.

This is reminiscent of numbers that are "multiplicatively closed" in some sense. Possibly the answer is that a_1 must be a multiple of 6 and have no proper divisor between N/6 and N/2 except possibly N/3? Let's try to find all fixed points by analyzing the possible values of (a,b) and M.

Let's define the set of exponents (α,β) that produce D/k > 1. For given (a,b), the condition D/k > 1 is 2^{α-(a-1)} 3^{β-(b-1)} t > M. Since t ≤ M, we can consider the worst-case t = M. Then we need 2^{α-(a-1)} 3^{β-(b-1)} > 1. So the set of (α,β) for which this product >1 is the set of points that are not in the "lower left" region where α ≤ a-1 and β ≤ b-1? But note that if α = a-1 and β = b-1, product = 1. So that gives D/k = t/M ≤ 1, so D ≤ k. If α = a-1 and β = b, product = 3, >1. So that's allowed. If α = a and β = b-1, product = 2, >1. If α = a and β = b, product = 6, >1. If α = a-2 and β = b, product = 2^{-2} * 3 = 3/4 <1. So that's not >1. If α = a and β = b-2, product = 2 * 3^{-1} = 2/3 <1. So the region where the product >1 is essentially when the point (α,β) is "large" in the sense that either α is large and β is not too small, or β is large and α is not too small. More precisely, define the line L: 2^{α-(a-1)} 3^{β-(b-1)} = 1 => α log2 + β log3 = (a-1)log2 + (b-1)log3. This line passes through (a-1,b-1) and also through (a,b-1)? Actually, at (a,b-1): left side = a log2 + (b-1)log3, right side = (a-1)log2 + (b-1)log3 = a log2 - log2 + (b-1)log3, so left side - right side = log2 >0, so product >1. So (a,b-1) is above the line. At (a-1,b): left side = (a-1)log2 + b log3, right side = (a-1)log2 + (b-1)log3, so difference = log3>0, so above. At (a,b): difference = log2+log3>0, above. At (a-2,b): left side = (a-2)log2 + b log3, right side = (a-1)log2 + (b-1)log3, difference = -log2 + log3 = log(3/2) >0? Actually, log3 - log2 >0, so product >1? Check: (a-2,b) with a=2,b=2 gives α=0,β=2, product = 2^{0-1}3^{2-1}=2^{-1}*3=1.5>1. So yes, product >1. So the region where product >1 is not simply α ≥ a-1 and β ≥ b-1; it's more like the points that are "northeast" of the line, but the line is curved in the exponent plane because it's exponential. However, we can still analyze by considering the integer comparisons.

Given that 2 and 3 are relatively prime, we can consider the possible values of the product 2^{α-(a-1)} 3^{β-(b-1)} as a rational number. For a given (a,b), the set of points (α,β) with 0≤α≤a, 0≤β≤b that give product >1 is finite. We need to ensure that for any such (α,β) with t = M (the maximal divisor of M), the resulting D = 2^α 3^β M is either N (if α=a,β=b) or one of the allowed ones (α=a,β=b-1) or (α=a-1,β=b). But what about (α,β) such that product >1 but not (a,b-1) or (a-1,b)? For example, (α,β)=(0,2) with a=2,b=2 gives product 1.5 >1. Then D = 2^0 3^2 M = 9M. Since k = 2^{1}3^{1}M = 6M, so D = 1.5k > k. And D is a proper divisor if (α,β)≠(a,b). So this would be a violation unless D equals 2k or 3k. But 9M is not 12M or 18M? Wait, 2k = 2*6M =12M, 3k=18M. So 9M is not allowed. Thus for (α,β) = (0,2), we get a violation unless maybe M is such that 9M is not a divisor? But it is because 2^0 3^2 M = 9M divides N = 4*9*M = 36M? Actually, N=2^2 3^2 M = 4*9*M =36M, and 9M divides 36M, yes. So 9M is a divisor. So to avoid violation, we must have that (α,β) = (0,2) does not produce D > k, i.e., we need 9M ≤ k = 6M, which implies M=0. So that's impossible. So the only way to avoid violation is to have that (α,β) = (0,2) is not a valid combination because perhaps α=0,β=2 is not allowed? But α can be 0,β=2, yes. So we need to ensure that for given (a,b), the only (α,β) that give product >1 are exactly the ones that correspond to 2k and 3k, and also possibly the case where t is not M but still D > k? So we need to restrict the possible (a,b) and M such that the set of divisors D > k is exactly {2k,3k}. This is a strong condition.

We can try to find all N with this property. Let's explore small N.

Compute for N up to, say, 100, the sum of three largest proper divisors, and see if it equals N.

(1,2): 18M = 2k? k=9M, 2k=18M, so allowed.
(1,3): 54M = N, not proper.
So the allowed are (1,3) gives N, (0,3) gives 3k, (1,2) gives 2k. So all t=M give allowed.

Now, consider t proper divisor of M. We need that for any (α,β) and any proper t|M, D ≤ k = 9M. The worst case is t=M itself, but we already handled. For t proper, we need to ensure that for (α,β) that could give D>9M, it doesn't happen. Which (α,β) can give D>9M? For t=M, we have (0,3) and (1,2) give D=27M and 18M, which are >9M. But those correspond to allowed ones. For t proper, D=2^α 3^β t. The only (α,β) that could give D>9M are those with 2^α 3^β > 9, because t ≤ M. For (α,β) = (0,3): 27, D=27t. For D>9M, need t > (9/27)M = M/3. So if there exists a proper divisor t of M with t > M/3, then D>9M. As argued, the only possible t > M/3 is t=M itself (since M/t<3). So no proper t > M/3. For (α,β) = (1,2): 18, D=18t. Need t > (9/18)M = M/2. No proper t > M/2. For (α,β) = (0,4) not allowed because β=4> b=3. For (α,β) = (2,0) not allowed. So no proper t can cause D>9M from these (α,β) because the condition t > M/3 or M/2 would require t=M. So safe.

But also, consider (α,β) = (0,2): 9, D=9t. For D>9M, need t>M, impossible. (α,β)=(1,1):6, D=6t, need t>1.5M, impossible. So no problem.

Thus for a=1,b=3, the condition holds if M is not divisible by 5? But we need to also check (α,β) = (0,3) with t proper? That gave D=27t, and we need to ensure that if D>9M, it is allowed. But D>9M occurs only if t> M/3, which only happens if t=M. So no proper t. So that's fine. However, what about (α,β) = (1,3)? So it seems that for a=1, any b≥1, the condition holds if M is not divisible by 5? But we also need to consider the case where a=1 and b=1 we already did; M not divisible by 5. Now, what about a=0? That would be N=3^b M. For b=1, N=3M. For M coprime to 6 and not divisible by 5? Let's test N=3*7=21: proper divisors 1,3,7. Sum of three largest? Thus we have an infinite family of fixed points.

But wait, are there any other fixed points not of this form? Could there be numbers where the three largest proper divisors are not exactly N/2, N/3, N/6? For example, N=30? N/2=15, N/3=10, N/6=5. But the three largest proper divisors of 30 are 15,10,6 sum=31>30, not equal. So not fixed. So the fixed point condition is that the three largest proper divisors sum to N. In our analysis, we assumed that the three largest proper divisors are N/2, N/3, N/6. But is it possible that the three largest are N/2, N/4, N/3? For N=12, the three largest are 6,4,3 sum=13. Thus the condition for N to be a fixed point is that N is divisible by 6 and that N/2, N/3, N/6 are the three largest proper divisors, and their sum is N. But the sum condition is automatically satisfied if these are the three largest? Not necessarily; we need the sum to be N. If N/2,N/3,N/6 are the three largest, then their sum is N*(1/2+1/3+1/6)=N. So yes, if these are the three largest proper divisors, then the sum is N. So the fixed point condition is equivalent to: N is a multiple of 6, and the three largest proper divisors of N are exactly N/2, N/3, N/6. That is a clean characterization.

Now, we need to determine all possible a_1 such that the sequence defined by a_{n+1}=sum of three largest proper divisors of a_n is infinite, i.e., each a_n has at least three proper divisors. So there are many numbers that eventually become fixed points. The question: Determine all possible values of a_1. So we need to characterize the set of all positive integers a_1 such that the sequence defined by a_{n+1}=f(a_n) is infinite (i.e., each term has at least three proper divisors). This is essentially the set of numbers that never hit a prime or a square of a prime. Since f is deterministic and we can analyze the dynamics. It might be that the only numbers that yield infinite sequences are those that are multiples of 6 and not multiples of 5? But 12 is multiple of 6 and not multiple of 5, yet fails. So that's not it.

Maybe the condition is that the number is divisible by 6 and not divisible by 4? But 150 is divisible by 2 but not 4, fails. So not that.

Thus the set of a_1 that work seems to be quite rich: all numbers that eventually map to a fixed point. Could it be that every number that is not of the form 2*3^k * M with M coprime to 6 and not divisible by 5 eventually hits a prime or a square of a prime? But we saw numbers like 72, 168, 192, 216, 264, 288, 504, 576, etc., all eventually map to fixed points. So the question: Determine all possible values of a_1. This is a known problem from some contest; I recall a problem: "A proper divisor of a positive integer N is a positive divisor of N other than N itself. The infinite sequence a_1, a_2, ... consists of positive integers, each of which has at least three proper divisors. For each n ≥ 1, the integer a_{n+1} is the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a_n. Determine all possible values of a_1." The answer is that a_1 must be a multiple of 6 and not a multiple of 5. Let's test: 12 is multiple of 6 and not multiple of 5, but fails. So that's not correct. Maybe it's that a_1 is not divisible by 5? 12 not divisible by 5 fails. So that's not it.

Perhaps the condition is that a_1 is not divisible by 4? 12 divisible by 4 fails; 24 divisible by 4 fails; 36 not divisible by 4 fails; 48 divisible by 4 fails; 60 not divisible by 4 fails; 84 divisible by 4 fails; 96 divisible by 4 fails; 120 divisible by 4? 120/4=30, so divisible by 4 fails; 144 divisible by 4 fails; 168 not divisible by 4? 168/4=42, so divisible by 4, but 168 works? Actually, we found 168 works. 168 is divisible by 4 (since 168=4*42), but 168 works. So "not divisible by 4" is not necessary.

Let's try to characterize the set of numbers that eventually map to a fixed point. Perhaps the set is all numbers that are not prime and not a square of a prime and not of the form 2^a * 3^b * p where p is a prime? No, that's too broad.

Given the complexity, I suspect that the answer is that a_1 must be a multiple of 6 and not a multiple of 5. But 12 fails, so maybe it's "multiple of 6 and not a multiple of 4"? 12 fails, 18 works, 24 fails, 30 fails (though 30 not multiple of 4), 36 fails, 42 works, 48 fails, 54 works, 60 fails, 66 works, 70 fails, 72 works? 72 is multiple of 4, so that condition fails. So not.

Maybe the condition is that a_1 is divisible by 6 and not by 5, and also not by 4? But 168 is divisible by 4 and works. So that's not.

Let's list successful a_1 we found: 6,18,42,54,66,78,102,114,126,138,168,174,186,192,198,216,222,234,258,264,282,288,294,306,318,324? Now, the recursion f(N) can produce squares of primes. For example, 24->26, not square. 26->16, which is 4^2 (square of prime 2). So 16 has only two proper divisors (1,2). So that stops. So we need to avoid N that eventually map to a square of a prime or a prime. So the set S is the set of N for which the orbit never hits a prime or a square of a prime. Thus we need a more systematic approach. Let's try to analyze the function f(N) in terms of the prime factorization. The three largest proper divisors of N are:

- If N is even: the largest proper divisor is N/2. The second largest could be N/3 if 3|N, else N/4 if 4|N, else N/5 if 5|N, etc. In general, for N even, the three largest proper divisors are: N/2, N/p where p is the smallest prime factor greater than 2? Not exactly, because if N is divisible by 3, then N/3 is a divisor, and it is less than N/2. If N is divisible by 4, then N/4 is a divisor, but could be larger than N/3? For N=12, N/2=6, N/3=4, N/4=3. So N/3 is larger than N/4. So the second largest is N/3 if 3|N, else N/4 if 4|N, else N/5 if 5|N, etc. But also, if N is divisible by 6, then N/6 is a divisor, but that's smaller than N/3. So the three largest are N/2, N/3, N/6 if 6|N. For N even and divisible by 3, N/3 is a divisor. If N is even and not divisible by 3, the second largest is N/4 if 4|N, else N/5 if 5|N, etc. However, if N is divisible by 4 but not by 3, then N/4 is a divisor. Could there be a divisor between N/2 and N/4? Possibly N/3 not integer, N/5 might be larger than N/4 if 5|N? No, N/5 < N/4 if N>0. So the ordering is: N/2 > N/3 > N/4 > N/5 > ... So the three largest proper divisors are: N/2, then either N/3 if 3|N, else N/4 if 4|N, else N/5 if 5|N, etc. Then the third largest is the next in that sequence after the second largest.

So for N even, f(N) = N/2 + d2 + d3, where d2 and d3 are the next two largest proper divisors. This depends on the prime factors.

Given the complexity, perhaps the problem has a simpler solution: Show that the sequence is eventually constant, and that the only possible constant values are numbers of the form 2*3^k * M with M coprime to 6 and not divisible by 5. Given the complexity, I think I should look for a more structural approach. Let's try to analyze the function f(N) more systematically. For any N, let d1 = N/p1, d2 = N/p2, d3 = N/p3, but these are not necessarily the three largest if N has small prime factors with exponents. Actually, the three largest proper divisors are the three largest numbers among all proper divisors. Since the set of proper divisors includes numbers that are N divided by any divisor >1. The largest proper divisor is N/2 if N even, else N/3 if N divisible by 3, else N/5, etc. So it's natural to define the "smallest prime factor" and then the next ones.

Let’s denote the prime factorization of N as N = ∏ p_i^{e_i}. The largest proper divisor is N/p_min, where p_min is the smallest prime factor. The second largest is either N/p_2 if p_2 is the second smallest prime factor, or N/p_min^2 if p_min^2 divides N? But note that N/p_min^2 = (N/p_min)/p_min. Since p_min < p_2, we have p_min < p_2, so N/p_min > N/p_2? Actually, N/p_min > N/p_2 because p_min < p_2. So N/p_min is the largest. The next candidate is N/p_2 if p_2 is the second smallest prime factor. If p_min^2 divides N, then N/p_min^2 is also a divisor. Compare N/p_min^2 and N/p_2: Since p_min^2 > p_min, we have N/p_min^2 < N/p_min. Also, p_min^2 may be less than p_2 or greater. Typically, p_min^2 could be less than p_2 if p_min=2, then 4<3? No, 4>3. So for p_min=2, p_min^2=4, and p_2 could be 3, so N/4 < N/3. So N/p_2 is larger than N/p_min^2. So the second largest is N/p_2 if 3|N, else N/p_min^2 if p_min^2|N and p_2>p_min^2? For N=12, p_min=2, p_2=3, N/3=4, N/4=3, so second largest is 4. For N=16, p_min=2, p_2=?? 16=2^4, so only prime 2, so second largest is N/4=4, third is N/8=2. So for N=16, the three largest are 8,4,2. So f(16)=8+4+2=14.

Thus the three largest proper divisors are determined by the smallest prime factor and the next smallest primes, and also the square of the smallest prime if it divides N.

So we need that no other combination gives a product in (1,3). This is a Diophantine condition. For given a,b, the possible (x,y) = (i-(a-1), j-(b-1)) with x from -(a-1) to 1, y from -(b-1) to 1.
